10.1016/j.jmp.2009.07.009
The purpose of this paper is to provide a new formal language for a class of multinomial processing tree (MPT) models, and to use the language to develop computationally efficient and useful representations as well as novel theorems for the class. MPT models are a popular class of information processing models for categorical data in specific cognitive paradigms. These models are designed to capture the way that latent cognitive processes interact to produce manifest categorical responses. MPT models have been used to disentangle competing theoretical explanations of particular cognitive phenomena, and they have been applied as measurement tools to compare cognitive capacities in the study of special populations.  Batchelder and Riefer (1999)  discuss over 80 applications of MPT models in various areas of cognitive psychology, and  Batchelder and Riefer (2007)  discuss many applications of the models to special populations.  Hu and Batchelder (1994) ,  Klauer (2006) ,  Knapp and Batchelder (2004) , and  Riefer and Batchelder (1988)  cover many of the mathematical and statistical properties of the models, and  Hu and Phillips (1999)  and  Stahl and Klauer (2007)  describe the general software that enable users to design their own models online, input categorical data, and conduct classical asymptotic inference using the results developed in  Hu and Batchelder (1994) .

MPT models share its characteristics with the class of tree models in statistical genetics ( Elandt-Johnson, 1971; Lange, 2002 , especially Chapter 10). First they are statistical models that parameterize a subset of the possible probability distributions on a finite set of categories. Second they are intentionally specified to reflect the simple, approximate, and substantive assumptions about their intended areas of application. Because of their simplicity, these models are open to scientific validation using standardized experimental designs. Further, and for purposes of this paper most importantly, they reflect a particular type of cognitive architecture represented as a rooted tree (see  Appendix ). MPT models have a recursive property that at any internal node of the tree, each continuation itself is an MPT model, with the node serving as the root. As we will see, this recursive property of MPT models motivates their description by a formal language.

In this paper we focus on a particular subclass of MPT models called  binary multinomial processing tree  (BMPT) models. This subclass is called binary because each internal node represents a processing choice point with two alternatives represented by two directed edges. This class covers the vast majority of applications of MPT models, and it has the useful property that any MPT model (see  Hu & Batchelder, 1994 ) is statistically equivalent (see  Definition 7 ) to a member of this class. The formalism we develop represents possible tree architectures as strings in a formal language. The main advantage of developing a string language to code BMPT models is that it facilitates the development of computationally efficient representations and algorithms that run on the strings of the language. Further the language will be seen to be useful in stating and developing proofs of some of the mathematical and statistical properties of the class. Researchers who design MPT models for scientific phenomena, for example those who use the software in  Hu and Phillips (1999) , may indeed find it more natural to represent their assumptions by assigning parameters and categories to the nodes of a tree represented in the usual way as a tree diagram (for example, see  Fig. 1 
                      ). However this representation is computationally inefficient to work with for large trees, especially when changes in the tree architecture are implemented to reflect hypotheses about the parameters (for example  Knapp & Batchelder, 2004 ). On the other hand, coding the MPT models by strings in a particular type of formal language provides not only a very succinct representation but it also has the extra benefit of being able to utilize the formal properties of the type of language involved. In particular, these benefits enable the construction of algorithms that are useful in representing, enumerating, modifying, and comparing models.

As we will show, every BMPT model can be viewed as assigning parameters and observable categories to the nodes of a particular type of rooted tree structure called a full binary tree. Each member of this class of tree structures can be represented uniquely as a string in a formal context-free language. We provide recursive axioms that interpret (provide semantics for) the strings consistent with the computational rules for the tree representation of BMPT models. Further, we develop algorithms that run on the string representation of a model that exhibit the submodels in the tree representation and compute the category probabilities as functions of the model parameters. A special subclass of BMPT models consists of models where no parameter or category is used more than once. Since every BMPT model is nested in at least one of these models, their enumeration and statistical properties are of interest. When parameters but not the categories of one of these BMPT models are allowed to repeat, the resulting collection of BMPT models forms a family of categorical models nested in the original model with interesting mathematical and statistical properties. Finally, when a researcher wants to place certain dimension reducing (see  Hu & Batchelder, 1994 ) or order constraining restrictions (see  Knapp & Batchelder, 2004 ) on the parameters of a BMPT model represented as a tree, algorithms can be constructed that automatically change the corresponding string representation to reflect the restrictions in a new, unrestricted BMPT model that is statistically equivalent to the old restricted model.

The remainder of the paper consists of six sections that provide the details. In Section  2  we revisit the usual definition of BMPT models represented as parameterized full binary trees, and we focus on the recursive nature of this representation. Then we give a formal axiomatization of BMPT models that provides a more succinct representation. It is shown that this axiomatization implies that each BMPT model can be uniquely coded as a string of category and parameter symbols. Section  3  is designed to show the connection between the tree and string representations of BMPT models in Section  2 . It starts by reviewing the definition of a context-free grammar. Then we axiomatize a context-free grammar for BMPT models and show that the string language revealed in Section  2  is closely related to the context-free language generated by this grammar. Then we review relevant results in the computer science literature concerning the coding of full binary trees and show that one such coding is equivalent to the BMPT context-free language. This result provides a link between the structural parts of the two definitions of BMPT models in Section  2 . In Section  4 , we develop semantics (model theory) for the BMPT language and show how the language can be said to meaningfully represent actual BMPT models. Section  5  discusses two special types of BMPT models. For each of these, we discuss their meaningfulness, their enumeration, relevant algorithms, and model identifiability. In Section  6  we give a high level description of a MATLAB program that is based on the ideas in this paper. In the last section, we offer concluding thoughts and ideas for future work.

Before proceeding with a new formalism of BMPT models, it is useful to present and formally sharpen the more conventional presentation of BMPT models in terms of full binary trees (e.g.  Batchelder & Riefer, 1999; Hu & Batchelder, 1994; Knapp & Batchelder, 2004 ). Each BMPT model has a structural and a computational component. The structural component is specified by the assignment of observable categories and latent parameters to the nodes of a  full binary tree  (FBT).  Appendix  describes these structures in detail, but in essence a FBT can be viewed as a special type of directed graph consisting of a finite set of nodes and a set of directed edges or arrows, where each arrow is directed out of one node and into another node. To characterize FBTs, we need the concepts of a  parent  and a  child  of a node. A parent of a particular node  
                            v 
                          is any node with an arrow directed into  
                            v 
                          and a child of  
                            v 
                          is any node that receives an arrow directed out of  
                            v 
                         . A FBT has a single node called the root with no parents, a set of terminal nodes (called leaves) with no children, and satisfying the properties that every node but the root has exactly one parent and every node that is not a leaf (internal node) has exactly two children.  Fig. 1  shows an example of a FBT in the left panel.

By convention a FBT is oriented downward with the root at the top as depicted in the figure. Consequently, each internal node has a left child and a right child. The usual definition of FBTs (see  Appendix ) allows digraphs with a single node and no arrows to be FBTs, where of course the single node is both the root and a leaf. These ‘atomic’ FBTs are important for the formalism in the paper.

The specification of a BMPT model assigns one of the categories to each leaf of the tree, and to each node that is not a leaf is assigned one of the component parameters,  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  s 
                               
                            
                         , where the left arrow from the node is associated with  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  s 
                               
                            
                          and the right arrow is associated with its ‘complement,’  
                            
                               ( 
                               1 
                               − 
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     s 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                          (this will be explained shortly). In general, categories and parameters can be used more than once in parameterizing the nodes of a model’s tree. A BMPT model obtained by parameterizing the FBT in the left panel of  Fig. 1  is presented in the right panel. This model is the well known pair-clustering model (e.g.  Batchelder & Riefer, 1980, 1986, 1990 ); however for our purposes we do not need to describe its substantive content.

In the literature on BMPT models, each internal node may be associated with a fixed numerical value  
                            x 
                            ∈ 
                            
                               ( 
                               0 
                               , 
                               1

                          instead of one of the parameters; however, for simplicity in this paper we only allow parameters in our formal description of the class of BMPT models. Including the possibility of numerical values at internal nodes does not add any formal nuances to our developments, and they can be regarded as setting a parameter in a BMPT model to a numerical probability while conducting statistical inference.

The computational component of a BMPT model defines a parameterized collection of probability distributions over its  
                            K 
                          categories. Any BMPT model consists of a finite set of branches, where each branch is a path (sequence of nodes and directed arrows) from the root to one of the leaves assigned to (terminating in) a category. The BMPT tree in right panel of  Fig. 1  has six branches, where categories  
                            
                               
                                  C 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  C

                                  C 
                               
                               
                                  4 
                               
                            
                          each have two branches. The computational rules for a BMPT imply that on any trial a single branch is selected probabilistically by a sequence of latent binary events starting with the root and ending in a leaf. The category assigned to the leaf at the end of the selected branch is the categorical observation for that trial. The branch probabilities are given in terms of the parameters assigned to the nodes of the tree. The first binary event is determined by whether the left child or the right child is chosen from the root node. If the root is assigned the parameter  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  s 
                               
                            
                         , then the left arrow is taken to the left child with probability  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  s 
                               
                            
                          and the right arrow is taken to the right child with probability  
                            
                               ( 
                               1 
                               − 
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     s 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                         . Each subsequent binary choice involves the node determined by the sequence of previous binary choices, and the choice at that node is probabilistically governed by the parameter assigned to it in the same way as for the root node. Thus the parameter assigned to any internal node  
                            ν 
                          can be interpreted as the conditional probability of taking the left arrow to the left child of the node given that previous binary choices have led to node  
                            ν 
                         . Given these computational rules, we are now in a position to characterize the probability distributions entailed by a BMPT model.

The previous definition of BMPT models is the one that has been implemented in the inference software program of  Hu and Phillips (1999)  which is in wide use among those who analyze data with MPT models. Users design their model online as a tree, assign parameters and categories, submit appropriate categorical data sets, and conduct statistical inference. The trees that are designed are often bulky, more than fill the computer screen, and are difficult to modify to represent parametric constraints. Consequently it is of interest to find a more efficient coding of BMPT models which we do it this section.

It is possible to capture in a succinct fashion the entire family of BMPT models described in Section  2.1  with five simple axioms. The result is that each BMPT model is represented as a string of parameter and category symbols. The key to our approach is the observation that every internal node of a FBT corresponds to a FBT with that node as the root. This property is exploited in the axioms by showing how BMPT models can be viewed as constructed from individual parameters and primitive ‘atomic’ models represented by single categories. Models that are not atomic are obtained by selecting any parameter  
                            θ 
                          and any two BMPT models  
                            M 
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          and constructing a new model by the string  
                            θ 
                            M 
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                         . The intended connection of this string representation to the definition in Section  2.1  is exhibited as a tree diagram in  Fig. 2 
                         ; however, the representation  
                            θ 
                            M 
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          in the axioms is to be viewed as a string of symbols (read from left to right with no punctuation between symbols) and not as a tree diagram.

Axiom 1  asserts that single categories are ‘atomic’ BMPT models. Technically these single category models do not satisfy the BMPT definition in Section  2.1 , but it is useful to include them in the recursive definition. Probabilistically they represent the case where the category represented by the atom occurs with probability one.  Axiom 2  shows how to recursively construct additional members of the class. This axiom represents a constructed model as a string rather than as a tree.  Axiom 3  states that any BMPT model can be deconstructed into a string consisting of three components, namely a parameter followed by two BMPT models, and so all BMPT models are either single categories or constructible by  Axiom 2 . There are several alternative and simpler ways that  Axiom 3  might have been written to close the class  
                            
                               B 
                            
                          to any model that cannot be constructed by  Axioms 1 and 2 ; however,  Axiom 3  is set up to facilitate the statement of  Axiom 5  which provides a recursive rule for generating probability distributions entailed by a BMPT model by decomposing it into its underlying constituent models. Notice that  Axiom 3  does not state that the decomposition of a model is unique. We show that this decomposition is necessarily unique in  Proposition 2  which uses  Proposition 1 . So before discussing the response axioms we need to present these two propositions.

Proposition 2  has important implications for the set  B  in  Definition 1 . First it states that the decomposition of a BMPT model in  Axiom 3  is unique. This allows  Axiom 5  to apply without ambiguity in computing category probabilities. In addition, the proposition implies that repeated application of  Axiom 3  to any member  
                            M 
                          in  
                            
                               B 
                            
                          leads to a decomposition of  
                            M 
                          into a unique subset of models, each characterized by a string of parameter and category symbols.

Section  2  provided two ways to define BMPT models, one as parameterized FBTs and the other as formal strings of parameter and category symbols. However, with the exception of the analysis of the model in  Fig. 1 , there was no effort to formally tie the two definitions together. The key to the connection between the definitions is to show that the structural parts of BMPT models are captured in an equivalent way by both definitions. First we consider  Definition 1  in Section  2.2 . It will turn out that the structural axioms, namely  Axioms 1–3  of  Definition 1 , can be represented in a context-free grammar (CFG) which is a standard structure in mathematical linguistics described in the next subsection. In Sections  3.2 and 3.3  we will develop formal CFGs, respectively, for BMPT models and FBTs, but before we can proceed we need to review some standard definitions.

A  formal language  in mathematical linguistics is defined as a (finite or infinite) set of strings of  symbols  constructible in accord with some system of formation rules (e.g.  Chomsky, 1963; Hopcroft & Ullman, 1979; Partee, Meulen, & Wall, 1990 ). Symbols are taken to be primitive (undefined, abstract) entities in the language like points and lines in a formal geometry or members of an alphabet for constructing words in a natural language. The alphabet of a formal language is any finite set of symbols, where some of the symbols of the alphabet may be endowed by formation rules to have a special meaning. A  string over an alphabet is a finite sequence of symbols from the alphabet juxtaposed from left to right, which means that they are written next to one another and without any punctuation like parentheses or commas. It is useful to include the  empty string ,  
                            ε 
                         , which is the string consisting of no symbols. The  length  of a string  
                            w 
                          is the number of symbols in the string (a symbol can be used multiple times in a string) and is denoted by  
                            
                               | 
                               w 
                               | 
                            
                         , therefore  
                            
                               | 
                               ε 
                               | 
                            
                            = 
                            0 
                         .

Formal languages have associated formal grammars consisting of  productions  (or  grammar rules ) that  generate  the set of strings of the language. The productions can be as simple as an extensional listing of all the strings in a finite language; however, most interesting formal languages have infinitely many strings and the productions have a recursive character. It is the recursive productions that make formal languages useful in the study of the properties of BMPT models since these models have a recursive structure as was exhibited in Sections  2.1 and 2.2 . While there are many possible formal grammars, much of the study in mathematical linguistics centers on the so-called Chomsky hierarchy of grammars ( Chomsky, 1959, 1963 ), where grammars are ordered by their generative power and each class of grammars (e.g. regular, context-free, context-sensitive, and recursively enumerable) that generate languages is associated with a type of automaton (e.g. finite automaton, pushdown automaton, linear-bounded automaton, and Turing machine), that accepts strings and determines if they are in the language generated by the grammar. In order to relate the two definitions of BMPT models in Section  2 , the class of  context-free grammars  (CFGs) is needed. Associated with any CFG is a  context-free language  (CFL) and an associated  pushdown automaton  (PDA). The CFG generates the language, and correspondingly the language that is generated by a context-free grammar is called a CFL. The associated PDA receives as input strings from the alphabet of the language and determines in each case if the string is one that can be generated by the associated CFG. In other words, one can think of a CFG as a generator of a CFL and a PDA as a CFL checker.

Before proceeding, it is necessary to define a unary operation defined on sets of strings called the  Kleene star operation  (e.g.  Hopcroft & Ullman, 1979 ). Let  
                            A 
                          be a set of finite length strings from a finite set of alphabet symbols. The Kleene star operation applied to  
                            A 
                          yields the set of strings defined by 
                            
                               (8) 
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        A 
                                     
                                     
                                        ∗ 
                                     
                                  
                                  = 
                                  
                                     { 
                                     
                                        
                                           s 
                                        
                                        
                                           1 
                                        
                                     
                                     
                                        
                                           s 
                                        
                                        
                                           2 
                                        
                                     
                                     … 
                                     
                                        
                                           s 
                                        
                                        
                                           k 
                                        
                                     
                                     | 
                                     0 
                                     < 
                                     k 
                                     < 
                                     ∞ 
                                     
                                     & 
                                     
                                     ∀ 
                                     i 
                                     ≤ 
                                     k 
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           s 
                                        
                                        
                                           i 
                                        
                                     
                                     ∈ 
                                     A 
                                     } 
                                  
                                  ∪ 
                                  
                                     { 
                                     ε 
                                     } 
                                  
                                  . 
                               
                            
                          Thus when the Kleene operation is applied to any set of strings  
                            A 
                          it generates a set  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          consisting of all elements obtained by concatenating any number of strings in the set  
                            A

                            ε

                                  A 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . Now we are in a position to define CFGs.

A CFG is designed to generate a CFL, and we need some additional discussion to indicate how this occurs. The alphabet of the CFL consists of the terminals of the CFG, namely  
                            Σ 
                         , and the CFL generated by the CFG is a subset of  
                            
                               
                                  Σ 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . The members of the CFL are those strings that can be generated by (derived from) the CFG. The way a string of the CFL is derived is by first taking the start variable  
                            S 
                          and then applying a sequence of productions leading to a string in the CFL. The first of these productions must be of the form  
                            S 
                            → 
                            A 
                         , and then  
                            S 
                          is replaced by the string  
                            A 
                         . For the next step in the derivation, some variable  
                            α 
                          in  
                            A 
                          is selected and then any ‘matching’ production of the form  
                            α 
                            → 
                            B 
                          can be applied. To illustrate suppose  
                            A 
                            = 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            … 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  − 
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            α 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  + 
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            … 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  n 
                               
                            
                         , where each  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  i 
                               
                            
                            ∈ 
                            V 
                            ∪ 
                            Σ 
                          and  
                            α 
                            ∈ 
                            V 
                         . Then when the production  
                            α 
                            → 
                            B 
                          is applied the result is the string  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            … 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  − 
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            B 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  + 
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            … 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  n 
                               
                            
                         , where  
                            B 
                          is itself a string in  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     V 
                                     ∪ 
                                     Σ 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         . In other words, the production  
                            α 
                            → 
                            B 
                          means that whenever in a derivation we see the variable  
                            α 
                          in a string, we can replace it with the string  
                            B 
                         . This process of taking any variable and applying a matching production is continued until the resulting string has no variables left in it. Such a string consisting only of terminals is called a  word  of the CFL generated by the grammar. The CFL itself consists of all words composed of only terminal symbols that can be obtained by following the process just described. Thus, from this point on in the paper we make a distinction between strings and words, where every word is a string (where each symbol is a terminal), but not every string is a word. CFGs have the essential property that productions can apply to a variable irrespective of its context (surrounding symbols); and for that reason these grammars are called context-free.

It is possible to capture the structural BMPT axioms of  Definition 1  in the form of a CFG, and the resulting CFL will consist of strings representing all possible structures of BMPT models. The main importance of having a formal language for BMPT models is that it captures the recursive aspect of BMPT in terms of productions in a context-free language, and this allows us to use results in the formal theory of language to discover properties of BMPT models. To facilitate the development of the formal language, we will use a single generic terminal symbol  
                            θ 
                          to represent parameters and another generic symbol  
                            C 
                          to represent categories in  Definition 1 . For example, in the CFL to be developed, the model in  (7)  will now be represented by the word  
                            θ 
                            θ 
                            C 
                            C 
                            θ 
                            θ 
                            C 
                            C 
                            θ 
                            C 
                            C 
                         . To get the actual model depicted in  (7)  and compute probabilities one would first make appropriate substitutions for the parameter and category symbols to get  (7)  and then apply the two response axioms of  Definition 1  to get the parameterized category probabilities. Definition 3  states the productions for a context-free grammar that produces words that represent the structural components of BMPT models as described in the first three axioms of  Definition 1 .

When we compare the structural axioms of  Definition 1  and the CFG in  Definition 3 , there are some apparent similarities. The first production is used to change the variable into a terminal  
                            C

                            M 
                          with a terminal parameter symbol followed by two variable model symbols. The preceding derivation of the model follows a ‘left first’ rule in that the variable that is used for the next production is always the left most variable in the currently produced string. In general there are many derivations of each word in a CFL, but for some CFGs we will see the left first rule has some useful properties.

The key to relating the structural parts of the two definitions of BMPT models in Sections  2.1 and 2.2  is found in the computer science literature on efficient codings of binary trees (see  Appendix  for a definition of a binary tree). The main purpose of coding binary trees is to provide succinct computer representations of the trees and to enable their enumeration. There are many codings of binary trees in the literature (see  Xiang, Tang, & Ushijima, 1997 , for a review) and several of them are developed as CFLs. The most relevant is discussed in  Mäkinen (1998) , where he proposes a CFG called

                            2

It is obvious that the grammars in  Definitions 3 and 4  are isomorphs of each other in the sense that if the symbols in

                            b 
                          as a leaf.  Mäkinen (1998)  shows that one can code any FBT as a string in  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  F 
                                  B 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                          by carrying out a  preorder traversal  of the tree. In general a traversal of a binary tree is an algorithm that visits each node of the tree exactly once in some order. Informally a preorder traversal is a traversal that starts with the root and performs the following operations recursively:  
                            
                               1. 
                               visit the root, 
                            
                            
                               2. 
                               traverse the left subtree, 
                            
                            
                               3. 
                               traverse the right subtree. 
                            
                         
                         Appendix  presents an algorithm for a preorder traversal of a FBT. As the traversal of a FBT takes place each node is classified as an internal node ( 
                            a 
                         ) or a terminal node ( 
                            b 
                         ), and the result is a string of  
                            a 
                            s 
                          and  
                            b 
                            s 
                          that code the tree.

visit the root,

traverse the left subtree,

traverse the right subtree.

To see this we need to review the combinatoric definition of the  Catalan numbers . These numbers are ubiquitous in enumerative combinatorics, in particular with recursively defined objects like trees, formal languages, the solution to Ballot Problem, etc. (e.g.  Stanley, 1999 , pp. 204–05). In particular, for each integer

The fact that despite their different motivations, the two formalisms in  Definitions 3 and 4  so closely correspond is based on the unanticipated fact that both can be viewed as preorder traversals of parameterized FBTs as formulated in Section  2.1 . In the case of  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  F 
                                  B 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                         , the preorder traversal generates a string of internal and leaf nodes, and in the case of  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                         , a preorder traversal of a parameterized FBT can also generate a string of parameters and categories, which are the objects assigned, respectively, to the internal and leaf nodes. Of course  Definition 1  was not constructed with preorder traversals of FBTs in mind, so it is interesting that they lead to the same formalism as well.

In this subsection we consider  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                          as a language and take up the semantics associated with words in the language. Informally  semantics  in formal language theory, mathematical logic, and computer science is concerned with giving an interpretation to a formal system. Semantics is sometimes handled formally as  model theory  and is generally given in set-theoretic terminology concomitant with Tarski’s definition of truth (e.g.  Tarski & Vaught, 1956 ); however, due to the nature of this paper, it is sufficient to describe the semantics for  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                          informally. In fact many aspects of the semantics have been discussed already in Section  2 ; consequently this subsection will not be extensive. Nevertheless it is necessary to associate the notions from Section  2  involving actual BMPT models to the words in  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                          because this was not the starting point in either definition of BMPT models in that section. There are two aspects to the semantics for  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                         . First, we need to hook the language of BMPT models onto words in  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                          by making the language interpretable with respect to parameters and categories. Second, we need to connect the language to a probabilistic tree semantics that generates probability distributions over the categories as a function of the parameters.

The groundwork for the first aspect for the semantics has already been laid in the introduction and in Section  2 . In particular, we informally defined two aspects of the semantics. First observable categories are ways that behavioral acts can be measured and classified, and second functionally independent latent parameters each with space (0, 1) are defined in the usual statistical sense. Given a word  
                            W 
                          in  
                            
                               
                                  L 
                               
                               
                                  B 
                                  M 
                                  P 
                                  T 
                               
                            
                          one can obtain an actual BMPT model for a situation involving categorical data by replacing each category symbol  
                            C 
                          in  
                            W 
                          with an observable category and each parameter symbol  
                            θ 
                          with a suitably defined latent parameter.  Definition 5  describes the conditions for such a valid substitution.

In addition, it is a simple exercise using  Propositions 1 and 2  to show that the possible strings in  Definition 1  are also characterized by  (10)  under the condition that a single generic category symbol and a single generic parameter symbol replace, respectively, categories and parameters in the strings. From these facts the proposition is obvious.  □

There is one nuisance to the semantics that requires comment. If a parameter is used more than once in a string in  
                            
                               B 
                            
                         , then the meaning of that parameter is different in each internal node in the tree that it is assigned to. While the probability calculations are blind to this distinction in connotations, the distinction is important for a full semantic understanding of this class of models. In particular if a parameter is assigned to an internal node, then its meaning is that of a conditional probability, and the condition can change as a function of where it occurs as an assignment to an internal node of the model’s FBT. In particular the meaning of a parameter assigned to an internal node  
                            ν 
                          is the probability that the left child of that node is taken given the condition that the previous binary choices have led to node  
                            ν 
                         . While the meaning of the parameter varies with the node it is attached to, when a particular numerical assignment is made to create a possible probability distribution over categories, the same number is replaced for each copy of the repeating parameter regardless of which node it is assigned to.

We have now seen that the class of BMPT models  
                         
                            B 
                         
                       represented as strings in  Definition 1  corresponds exactly to the class  F  of BMPT models represented as a parameterized FBTs as described in Section  2.1 . More formally there is a bijection between  
                         
                            B 
                         
                       and  F  that is easily constructed by the concepts in the previous sections. In addition corresponding to each such model is a unique word in  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                       from which the model can be obtained by valid substitutions of observable categories and latent parameters as described in  Definition 5 . There are three interesting subclasses of BMPT models that can be defined by placing the restriction on the substitutions. The first class requires that no parameter or category repeats, and we refer to this class as  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  u 
                               
                            
                         
                      . The second class requires that no category can repeat but it places no restrictions on the possible duplications of parameters, and we refer to this class as  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  uc 
                               
                            
                         
                      . Clearly we have  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  u 
                               
                            
                         
                         ⊂ 
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  uc 
                               
                            
                         
                         ⊂ 
                         
                            B 
                         
                      . The third class is a special subclass of models in  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  uc 
                               
                            
                         
                       that have only one parameter denoted by  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  uc 
                               
                               1 
                            
                         
                      . For these three subclasses of models we discuss the following three issues: (1) enumeration of the models; (2) the statement of when these models are identifiable and when pairs of these models can be said to be statistically equivalent (concepts that we will define shortly); and (3) the computational algorithms that generate  CP(M)  in  (12)  for any model in the class. We take up the computational algorithms for these and other models in  
                         
                            B 
                         
                       in Section  6 .

The class  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     B 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     u 
                                  
                               
                            
                          consists of BMPT models that use each category and each parameter only once. Since these models have both unique categories and unique parameters, they can be viewed as the master class of BMPT models since any BMPT model can be obtained from a model in  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     B 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     u 
                                  
                               
                            
                          by duplicating one or more of its parameters and/or categories. Thus, the fundamental importance of this subclass of BMPT models is that every other type of BMPT model can be viewed as a modification of this type of model and so if we determine properties of this class, we can then modify them for other models that are more obviously cognitively relevant.

Consider a word  
                               W 
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  
                                     L

                                     M

                                     T

                               m

                               2 
                               n

                               n 
                               = 
                               1

                               2

                                  B

However, there is another perspective of enumerating the models in  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        u 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             that is more interesting and it ties to the use of evolutionary trees in statistical genetics.

As was mentioned in the introduction, MPT models share characteristics with the class of tree models in statistical genetics. An essential idea in molecular phylogeny is to infer the evolutionary relationships among related taxa, where taxa are species, genera, families, or higher groupings ( Lange, 2002 , p. 199). The taxa, under the assumption that the DNA sequences for the taxa have been successfully aligned, are then arranged in an  evolutionary 
                            tree  (or  phylogeny ) that depicts how taxa diverge from common ancestors. The evolutionary tree is represented by a full binary tree and so under these genetic assumptions, the root represents the common ancestor of all of the taxa. Further, the terminal nodes are contemporary taxa and the internal nodes are  speciation events , so-called since they are where new taxa bifurcate ( Lange, 2002 , p. 200). With this theoretical background, geneticists take a body of observational data and use various statistical methods to determine the best tree as well as the lengths of the branches of the tree. So in the phylogenetic case it is  a priori  clear what the internal nodes represent. Once the words of  
                               
                                  
                                     L 
                                  
                                  
                                     B 
                                     M 
                                     P 
                                     T 
                                  
                               
                             receive a semantic interpretation as a probabilistic tree structure as was done in Section  4.2 , intermediate nodes can be interpreted in a way that is similar to that of the statistical genetics categories of contemporary taxa.

In the case of a model  
                               M 
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        u 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             of length  
                               
                                  | 
                                  M 
                                  | 
                               
                               = 
                               m 
                               = 
                               2 
                               n 
                               + 
                               1 
                            , let  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        C 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                               
                             denote the set of  
                               n 
                               + 
                               1 
                             unique categories of  
                               M 
                            . Suppose each of these categories has a different cognitive meaning. Because of this there is a sense of enumeration that is different from the one achieved by enumerating all the models in  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        u 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             of length  
                               m 
                            . In particular each internal node  
                               ν 
                             of the tree can be associated with the subset of the categories that are assigned to leaves that are on branches that transit through  
                               ν 
                            . The two children of  
                               ν 
                             partition (split) this subset of categories into two subsets, namely the ones that are leaves on branches of the tree that include the left child of that node and the ones that are leaves on branches that include the right child. For example, the initial node partitions the entire set of categories into two sets, namely those that follow from its left child and those which follow from its right child. Then these two subsets of categories are further split into two subsets each by the two children of each of these nodes. This process continues until no further splits are possible and single categories appear as leaves of the tree. Every pair of categories  
                               
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                  
                               
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     C 
                                  
                                  
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        C 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                               
                             has a unique internal node  
                               
                                  
                                     ν 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                             where they first separate into different cells of the binary partition of that node, and part of the cognitive meaning of each internal node is entailed in the fact that it represents a cognitive process that partitions a particular set of categories.

This description of the way a BMPT model separates sets of categories by cognitive processes is relevant to an important aspect of MPT modeling, namely model validation (e.g.  Batchelder & Riefer, 1999; Schweickert & Chen, 2008 ). The way that MPT models are validated is by conducting experiments that are intended to selectively influence the values of certain parameters while leaving others unaffected. If successful, these experimental manipulations alter category probabilities that in turn lead to changes in the estimates of the targeted parameters. In this way one can dissociate the parameters experimentally and argue that they are measuring what the purport to measure.

We believe that this approach to selectively influencing parameters is analogous to the way of determining taxa coming from the same ancestor. Further it motivates the question of the maximum number of members of  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        u

For now we want to take up two important and related issues in statistical inference, namely the identifiability of the parameters from data and the empirical distinguishability of two models.  Definition 6  describes when a BMPT model is said to be  globally identified , and  Definition 7  describes when two BMPT models are said to be  statistically equivalent .

Eqs.  (2) and (12)  show that corresponding to every numerical assignment to the parameters of a model there is a corresponding unique probability distribution over the categories. This is important in statistical inference because it means that the model parameter in principle can be uniquely recovered from knowledge of the category probability distribution. In general, a complete study of determining the identifiability properties of

Definition 7  says that statistically equivalent models entail exactly the same set of possible probability distributions over the same set of categories. If two models are statistically equivalent, they cannot be separated by knowledge of the category probabilities and therefore they cannot be empirically separated without auxiliary assumptions. The first step toward discovering whether or not two BMPT models,

                                        u 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             consists of BMPT models from which all other members of  
                               
                                  B 
                               
                             are special cases; the class itself does not have an interesting probabilistic semantics. This is because regardless of how the FBT is constructed for a model with  
                               K 
                             leaves, the model is  saturated  in the sense that the set of probability distributions entailed by the model is coextensive with the entire probability simplex  
                               
                                  
                                     Λ 
                                  
                                  
                                     K 
                                  
                               
                            .

As was mentioned in the beginning of this section a special subclass of the models in  
                            
                               B 
                            
                          are the models in  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     B 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     uc 
                                  
                               
                            
                          that have a unique categories but possibly repeated parameters. These models have only one branch associated with each observable category in the behavioral partition of the data; however, it is possible that the same parameter governs the probability at several internal nodes in the FBT for the model. A special subclass of these models has only a single repeated parameter, and this class has some interesting properties of its own. We denote this class by  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     B 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     uc 
                                  
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          and deal with it in a later subsection. Like we did for the more general case for  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     B 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  
                                     u 
                                  
                               
                            
                         , we present some results on enumerations, identifiability and statistical equivalence between such models, and algorithms for dealing with this class are presented in Section  6 .

Proposition 11  is interesting because global identifiability of models in  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        uc 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             is maintained even if parameters can repeat. The difference between this proposition and  Proposition 9  for the class  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     u 
                                  
                               
                             is that when parameters can repeat it is no longer the case that  
                               Λ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  M 
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               
                                  
                                     Λ 
                                  
                                  
                                     K 
                                  
                               
                            . In addition  Proposition 11  is related to the statistical approach to analyzing MPT models in  Hu and Batchelder (1994)  based on the so-called EM algorithm. The algorithm provides maximum likelihood estimates (M.L.E.s) even in the case of repeated categories as well as repeated parameters by alternating the M-step and the E-step of the algorithm. The E-step provides estimates of the unobservable branch frequencies in the case where categories can repeat, and these estimates are used to generate estimates of the M.L.E.s by using the fact that a model without repeated categories ( 
                               ∀ 
                               k 
                               , 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     I 
                                  
                                  
                                     k 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               1 
                             in  (1) ) has closed form M.L.E.s as suitable ratios of category probabilities as in the proof of  Proposition 9 .

It is possible to make progress in characterizing the conditions under which two models in  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        B 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        uc 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             are statistically equivalent by working with the form of  (15) . Basically  (15)  asserts that parameterized category probabilities are finite polynomials in the parameters. This places strong conditions on the types of reparameterizations that satisfy equivalence in  Definition 7 . We leave it as an open problem to completely characterize these conditions.
