We have constructed a program in MATLAB that has algorithms that implement many of the computations discussed in this paper, and it is currently a work in progress.

It is currently available at  http://sunny.moorparkcollege.edu/~bpurdy/algorithm.html , and when it is completed, it will be made generally available without cost to users. The MATLAB program begins by asking for a word in the BMPT context-free language ( 
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                      ) where  
                         T 
                       represents  
                         θ 
                       for parameters and  
                         C 
                       represents categories. If the “inputted” string has symbols other than  
                         T 
                       or  
                         C 
                       or if the string of  
                         T 
                       and  
                         C 
                       symbols is not a word in the language, then the program informs the user that the word is not in the BMPT language. The string checker in the program is based on the set-theoretic definition of the BMPT context-free languages. It has the form of  (10) , with replacements of  
                         T 
                       for  
                         a 
                       and  
                         C 
                       for  
                         b 
                      . In particular, it checks to see if the string satisfies the running count of the difference between parameter and category symbols required by  (10) .

If the word is found to be in  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                      , the program then breaks the word into submodels by repeated use of  Axiom 3  guided by the fact from  Proposition 2  that for any string in  
                         M 
                         ∈ 
                         
                            B 
                         
                      , the division between submodels is always the place ‘ 
                         i 
                      ,’ where  
                         
                            
                               S 
                            
                            
                               M 
                            
                         
                         
                            ( 
                            1 
                            , 
                            i 
                            ) 
                         
                       in  (6)  first has a value of zero. The program gives the user the opportunity to see a list of all of the submodels. The submodels are displayed as strings.

The program uses the submodels to create a vector that is used for the treeplot() function in MATLAB. The treeplot() function automatically generates the binary tree structure for the BMPT model. After the tree is generated the user has the opportunity to label the nodes of the tree and assign parameters and categories to the nodes. In terms of the paper, the program generates a 2D representation of a BMPT model from a word in the BMPT context-free language. Next the program generates branch probability distributions so that the user can input parameters and/or numbers in  
                         
                            ( 
                            0 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                       for parameters and observe the parameterized category probability distribution. In particular, the program will take the original word in  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                       and turn it into a model. This is done by an automatic procedure that labels the subscripts of  
                         T 
                       and  
                         C 
                       using the preorder algorithm for the  
                         T 
                         s 
                       (which are the internal nodes) as well as the  
                         C 
                         s 
                       (the external nodes). Of course, the  
                         C 
                         s 
                       can be simply relabeled from left to right in order instead of using the preorder algorithm. This will also modify the treeplot() diagram so that the subscripts are represented as well.

At this point the program will ask the user to input specific parameters or numbers and categories. This will again modify the treeplot() diagram and give the branch probabilities. Also, we are working on natural ways that the program can link to MATLAB statistical packages as well as ancillary programs like WinBUGS or R. At this juncture, it is good to point out that this program is portable such that it can work with an R statistical package for BMPT models that is under development.

Insofar as going (from 2D to 1D), the program can move from the vector that represents a tree and is the argument for treeplot() and work backwards. This involves the opposite procedure described in the first paragraph. In particular, the vector that is the argument for treeplot() is transformed into a word in  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                      . Also, if one has the string that represents a particular BMPT model, then the program simply pulls off the subscripts in order to get the word that represents the generic BMPT model with the same tree structure.

In this paper we formalized BMPT models in two different ways in Section  2 . In Section  2.1  we sharpened the usual way that BMPT models are defined as parameterized FBTs, but in Section  2.2  we axiomatized BMPT models as a set of strings of parameter and category symbols based on recursive formation axioms in  Definition 1 . We showed that this new formulation leads naturally to a CFL for BMPT models denoted as  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                      . Then we showed that  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                       is equivalent to a context-free language for finite preordered FBTs. The CFL was used to show that actual BMPT models were obtained from valid substitutions of parameters and categories into the symbols of the words of  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                      , and such substitutions led in a natural way to actual parameterized FBTs as described in Section  2.1 . Finally, we gave applications of  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               B 
                               M 
                               P 
                               T 
                            
                         
                       which allowed us to state enumerations and statistical equivalence for two subclasses of models, one denoted by  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  u 
                               
                            
                         
                       that consists only of BMPT models with unique parameters and categories and the other denoted by  
                         
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                            
                            
                               
                                  uc 
                               
                            
                         
                       that consists of BMPT models with unique categories but possible repeated parameters. In Section  6 , we described an algorithm (the BMPT Algorithm) that automatically generates the category probabilities from a string in  
                         
                            B 
                         
                      . In practice, one can have this algorithm call another program that will then estimate the latent parameters. For references to such programs for Bayesian modeling, see  Gill (2002)  or  Robert (2007) .

There are two main advantages of coding of BMPT models as strings rather than the usual way in terms of parameterized FBTs. First string codes are a very efficient representation both in terms of computer memory and in terms of transporting models from one computer to another. It is this same motivation that led computer scientists who use tree representations of data structures to seek efficient codings, and we have been able to utilize some of their ideas in our work. A second advantage to a string representation for BMPT models is that connecting BMPT models to CFLs allows the statement and proof of new propositions about this class of models. Examples of these propositions are the enumeration results in Section  5  and the results on statistical equivalences of models. Of course actual MPT modelers will not develop their models originally as strings, and instead they will draw trees; but then encoding the constructed model as a string will make transmission and statistical analysis of the model much more efficient.

With respect to future work, we hope to continue to prove statistically, and hence, psychologically, germane propositions as well as to develop algorithms for BMPT models. One obvious target is the work in  Knapp and Batchelder (2004) . In this work it was shown that if it is required in a BMPT model that one parameter, say  
                         
                            
                               θ 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      , is necessarily smaller than another parameter, say  
                         
                            
                               θ 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      , then the resulting model under the aforementioned order restriction is not a BMPT model because the parameters are not free to vary in (0,1). In  Knapp and Batchelder (2004)  it is shown how to create a BMPT model in the sense of Section  2.1 , where the number of parameters is identical, there are no constraints on the parameters, and the new model is statistically equivalent to the restricted BMPT model. That paper goes on to describe in precise detail how the new, statistically equivalent model can be constructed as a modification of the original model’s FBT. However, the string language in  
                         
                            B 
                         
                       is a much more succinct representation of BMPT models. One goal of future research will be to develop grammatical transformation rules that will accept as input a BMPT model, select one or more parameters, place restrictions on parameter space, and then generate as a transformation a new BMPT model that is statistically equivalent to the original model with its parameter restrictions.

In addition, it will be desirable to formulate future propositions about the identifiably and model selection properties of BMPT models in terms of their string representation. For example,  Wu, Myung, and Batchelder (unpublished)  analyze BMPT models from the viewpoint of minimum description length (MDL, see  Grünwald (2000) . MDL is based on shortest codes for data conditional on a model, and therefore it is a prime target to be discussed in terms of the string language in  Definition 1 . Another issue concerns MPT models that are not BMPT models. Such models allow also for either one or more than two children from an internal node. It is not difficult to see that any MPT model can be coded into a model in  B  by following well known efficient codings of trees (e.g.  Xiang et al., 1997 ). Also, we want to investigate the relationship between BMPT models and other well known probabilistic tree models that are used in the sciences, e.g. Bayesian networks ( Neapolitan, 2004 ). We believe that the formal succinct coding of BMPT models as strings based on capturing the structural component as a context-free language will facilitate the statement and proof of theorems in this effort.

The authors are grateful for the comments of Richard Schweickert, David Riefer, and an anonymous referee. The idea behind  Axiom 2  of  Definition 1  was suggested by Bethany Knapp. Work on this paper by the first author was supported by fellowships from the School of Social Sciences and the Institute for Mathematical Behavioral Sciences at UC Irvine. Work on this paper by the second author was supported on a grant from National Science Foundation: SES-0616657 to Xiangen Hu & William H. Batchelder (Co-PIs).

This appendix provides definitions leading to the concept of a  full binary tree  (FBT) and its properties. There are several approaches to defining the concepts in this appendix, some starting with graph theory (e.g.  Harary, 1994 ), some beginning with computer science (e.g.  Knuth, 1997 ), and others that emphasize mathematical linguistics (e.g.  Kracht, 2003; Partee et al., 1990 ). We choose an approach starting with directed graphs. A finite directed graph or  digraph  is a finite set of  nodes  with arrows or  directed edges  connecting some of the ordered pairs of distinct of nodes. We denote a digraph

                            } 
                         
                      , namely a irreflexive relation on  
                         V 
                      . To get to the concept of binary trees we further require  
                         E 
                       to be an asymmetric relation, that is

                               y

Definition A.1 
                         A  rooted tree  is an asymmetric digraph  
                               G 
                               = 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  V 
                                  , 
                                  E 
                                  )

                               ν

Further a  binary tree  is a rooted tree in which no node has more than two children, and a  full binary tree  (FBT) is a binary tree in which every node has either exactly two or exactly no children.

A  rooted tree  is an asymmetric digraph  
                               G

Further a  binary tree  is a rooted tree in which no node has more than two children, and a  full binary tree  (FBT) is a binary tree in which every node has either exactly two or exactly no children.

For our formalization of context-free language for full binary trees we also need a recursive data structure algorithm called the  preorder algorithm . In graph theory, moving through a tree in a prescribed order and visiting all of the nodes in the tree exactly once is called a  tree traversal . In particular, we want to capture the idea of a  depth-first traversal , i.e. a sequence of moves that traverses the depth of the tree first ( Main & Savitch, 2001 , pp. 456–457). There are three depth-first traversals: preorder, postorder, and inorder. With respect to FBTs, it is convenient, for our formalism, to adopt the  preorder traversal  ( Stanley, 1999 , p. 33). It is called the preorder traversal since it visits the root of each subtree before visiting any of the subtree’s other nodes.  Algorithm A.1  is implemented in programming languages like C++ and Java using data structures ( Main & Savitch, 2001 , pp. 479–483), ( Hubbard & Huray, 2004 , pp. 342–343). The preorder algorithm works on all single rooted trees, but for our case it will be used only for full binary trees.

The preorder algorithm can be described informally as follows:  
                         
                            1. 
                            Process the root. 
                         
                         
                            2. 
                            Traverse the left subtree, if it exists, with a preorder recursive call. 
                         
                         
                            3. 
                            Traverse the right subtree, if it exists, with a preorder recursive call.

Process the root.

Traverse the left subtree, if it exists, with a preorder recursive call.

Traverse the right subtree, if it exists, with a preorder recursive call.

Algorithm A.1 
                         Preorder Algorithm 
                         The  algorithm for preorder traversal  is as follows (in C++) ( Main & Savitch, 2001 , p. 482). 
                         
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                     
                                        
                                           preorder(tree) 
                                        
                                        
                                           { 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               if (node_ptr !  
                                                 = 
                                               NULL) 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               { 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               std::cout  
                                                 ≪ 
                                               node_ptr- 
                                                 > 
                                              data()  
                                                 ≪ 
                                               std::endl; 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               preorder_print(node_ptr- 
                                                 > 
                                              left() ); 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               preorder_print(node_ptr- 
                                                 > 
                                              right() ); 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               } 
                                        
                                        
                                           } 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                         Note that one can write the preorder code in other compiled languages like Java. Also, the preorder traversal can be implemented in array languages like MATLAB and functional languages like  Mathematica  (with the “DiscreteMath Combinatorica” package).

The  algorithm for preorder traversal  is as follows (in C++) ( Main & Savitch, 2001 , p. 482).

preorder(tree) 
                                        
                                        
                                           { 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               if (node_ptr !  
                                                 = 
                                               NULL) 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               {

                                                 > 
                                              left() ); 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               preorder_print(node_ptr- 
                                                 > 
                                              right() ); 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                               } 
                                        
                                        
                                           }

Note that one can write the preorder code in other compiled languages like Java. Also, the preorder traversal can be implemented in array languages like MATLAB and functional languages like  Mathematica  (with the “DiscreteMath Combinatorica” package).
